RATING: 8
[COLOR=#FFFFFF][BGCOLOR=#FF9933][b]aaj's FAVORITE SET COLLECTOR[/b][/BGCOLOR][/COLOR]
[I]tl;dr - gorgeous, relaxing artwork and a super-slim ruleset, along with a genius movement mechanic, make Tokaido a perfect-10 gateway game for me.[/i]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

First off, Tokaido is gorgeous; secondly, it's peaceful and enjoyable to play; and thirdly, despite a simple appearance there are a lot of decisions to make along the road. 

Players play as travelers along the famous Tokaido road, from Tokyo to Edo. As we progress throughout the game, we stop at souvenir shops, visit temples, enjoy hot springs, meet strange characters, paint beautiful panoramic pictures, and eat delicious meals. Each different experience is a way to earn points and add to your set of cards in front of you. 

I've tried a different specialty each time (temple, panoramas, souvenirs), and have not walked away with a victory every time. It's possible for first-time players to win this game, but there's also a lot of strategy underneath the movements as well. 

I enjoy Tokaido most with 4+ players, when the secondary slots open up. In 3-player, things are very constrained and strategy is even more important. 4-player is great for introducing people to the game. I've not yet tried 2-player.

There's something for everybody in Tokaido, though. Aside from describing the eight ways of earning victory points, this is an incredibly simple game to teach and explain. New players get hooked easily, and it's already become one of my own favorites.

RATING: 6
*Rating based on one play.

This is a very light, quick-playing game that is short enough not to overstay its welcome.  It feels a bit like Candyland for adults, but rather than moving ahead a random amount, the players get to choose how far ahead they want to go.  It's a clever idea and is well executed, but has some drawbacks that I think keep it from being much more than a decent game.

First, the fixed nature of the board seems like it would restrict replayability.  The different roles and random card draws might help mitigate the sameness between games, but I don't see this as a game I would want to play more than a few times.

Second, the board and component design leave something to be desired.  The board itself seems very sparse in appearance.  The spots on the board are very small and the various icons for the different actions are too similar to be easily distinguished.  There were lots of instances where players had to ask what a particular icon did.  There is a lot of white space on the board that makes it feel more like a prototype than a finished product.  And the score tokens are tiny!  We're talking about the size of pills.  These are seriously a swallowing hazard for children (though, on the plus side, they would probably go through quite easily without doing any harm) and would be very easy to lose.  The scoring track has a staggered layout that likely makes scoring errors very easy to make.

Finally, the last thing holding the game back for me is more subjective, and that is the theme.  I'm not much interested in Japanese culture, so the theme was quite "meh" for me.  However, my friend who owned the game is quite a Japanophile and seemed to really enjoy the theme, so take that for what you will.

All in all, the game plays quick and is interesting enough to be worth a few plays, but I don't expect it to hit the table all that often.  It's a decent filler, but not one of my favorites.

RATING: 7.5
Beautiful art design on the board, cards and pieces. People who appreciate aesthetics will be immediately drawn to the game to explore what it represents. 

Gameplay is incredibly light and mellow, but lengthy for as simple as it truly is. Great gateway game as the decisions and strategy are simple and few, but there's a very light layer of them to keep this from being ultimately mindless. For instance, if you're trying to collect a full panorama or the most hot spring cards, you have to choose jumping straight to those locations based on how many more stops are in the future, and risk bypassing other things you can collect. You also gamble if a spot further down the road will be available when it's your turn again, or if another player will have camped it out and forced you to bypass it. 

Plus, with the variety of ways to earn points for your final score, the winner isn't always immediately obvious, which can make for a fun surprise. That said, people looking for something deeper may find this too simple and therefore boring.

Something that's a great compliment to the game is I played it with an American friend who lived in Japan for three years as well as someone born and raised there who moved here in his twenties just six months ago. Both of them loved the contents of the game, and the native Japanese guy was delighted to see all the clothing, food and uses of Japanese language. Several times he said it made him nostalgic and maybe a bit homesick! 

Positives
- Beautiful art and graphic design
- Multiple ways to get points
- Easy to teach and play

Negatives
- Almost a little too light
- Takes a lot of time for as light as it is

RATING: 4.5
I played this game just once, so these are mostly impressions that may change after multiple plays.

I've heard so many good things about this game, about how it is "zen" and how it doesn't steal the focus from the conversation. In my first play, I don't know if Tokaido accomplished any of those things for me.

It's a cute set collection game with a very beautiful graphic presentation, but it's not my style of game. It's way too light for me. I guess the reason why people say this game allows for conversation is that the choices are so straight forward. In that case though you need the right group of people to play it, because this atmosphere breaks down as soon as there's a person who doesn't partake in it and looks like waiting for his/her turn. I guess that I would rather just have a conversation instead.

Therefore Tokaido is not a game for me. It is simply too light for my taste.

RATING: 6.5
It is not a bad game, another worker placement to play only one way line, like Egizia or Francis Drake. The difference here is the play order, that I didn't liked very much. It seems difficult to get out of a vicious cicle since you cannot go to far away from the ones behind because if so, you would not play for a while.

In egizia and Francis drake we have a different kind of approach that I like more, because the play order is fixed for the round and the only one way worker placement needs to prioritize the best places abdicting the first places and not the best positioned vicious cicle route.

Despite of this, I enjoyed the playtime and the rules are very simple, so good for teaching, but Bauza have other better games of his signature.

The minimalist art here is very beautiful.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Tokaido has a pile of cards and a line of action spaces you land on to... get cards. You collect cards. Build sets, get random effects, or just plain victory points depending upon the type of space/card you land on. There's also money that you need to buy certain cards and invest in the temple to get in on the ranking of 'most donated' at the end for bonus veeps. 

Overall, that's not very interesting and could maybe make for an okay or sub-par game. Where Tokaido tries to break the mold is by implementing a time track'esque movement mechanic. The person last in line on the board's path gets to go. So if you jump straight to the action you want to build up your set or make some money, you might get less actions. But if you take the action that lets you do the most, you might not end up doing what you want.

The only remaining problem is that the game's path is so short and simple, there's really only a handful (or less) points in the game you have to make any particularly hard decisions. Usually the most beneficial next placement is quite obvious. 

However, there is also a pile of special characters you get to choose from (out of two dealt) each game that all start with different money banks and special abilities. This at least gives you a new situation to think through every time you play. And the art on the game is very pleasing aesthetically.

RATING: 6
A game that reminds me of Knizia's Tutankhamun.  There is the same progression along a path, collecting sets of different things, judging whether to sprint ahead to be sure of that thing you most desire or take lots of small steps at the risk of missing out.  Tokaido adds a chunk of luck in the form of characters you meet along the way, who provide benefits of varying utility.  I did like the concept of the inns, at which everyone is obliged to stop, with those arriving last paying more for their meals.  The Japanese theming is quite nicely done too.

I found the artwork strikingly different and quite pleasing.  Production quality is good.

RATING: 8
Great board game!  It's easy to pick up, has the right balance of skill and luck to keep things interesting, and I *love* the "furthest back goes next" feature, giving you an interesting decision:  Do you jump ahead and claim what you want, or hang back and potentially get to take more turns?  The theme is pretty fun, too.

My only reason for not ranking the game higher is that some of the travelers have much more useful abilities than others, with some being easy to block while others have abilities that are unblockable (the one that allows you to get a friend anytime you are at an inn comes to mind; that means you're guaranteed 4 friends).  This can lead to a bit of an imbalance, but it's a minor concern.

RATING: 7
Tokaido is a set collecting game with an interesting theme.  In Tokaido each player is simply taking a walk from Kyoto to Edo (now Tokyo).  It is the player who has the most enjoyable and fulfilling experience walking this legendary path who wins the game.

As card draws are random, fate plays a large role in the game.  It is the player who best accepts (manages) his fate who will have the most fulfilling journey.

RATING: 10
Played at Essen. Beautiful game with a design and rythm of play close to its theme.

I would have like to play more but Funforge booth was crowded. I looked also other people playing to determine the balance of the game. It appears that the players have to carefully determine their movement depending of the character they play. As the players can move exactly as they wish there is few luck elements. The events during the game are always positive, therefore, most of the gameplay is based on the movement, yours and the movement of the other player who can block you sometimes.

Real cool game from Antoine Bauza and gorgious artworks. Deserve a 10 for sure

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In this game, players are vacationing in the countryside of Japan; collecting paintings, taking baths in hot springs, and meeting new people along the way. This is somewhat of a point-salad using a simple "rondel", meaning players can choose to go to a spot nearer or farther but cannot go backwards. Players move from one end of the map to the other, and the spaces they choose to go to can either let them work on a painting, take a hot spring bath with some monkeys, collect some souvenirs, or maybe even collect a little spending money as needed. The balance here is whether to go a little farther to get what you need, or to slow down and try to hit as many things as possible.

The decisions are interesting and there are many paths to victory, but this game's theme and artwork give a very relaxing and enjoyable vibe. Also, it's simple enough to possibly be a gateway game for those that have played some big name games like monopoly or life as well. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
While the imagery is quite remarkable, and the game seems well-balanced, I don't think there is enough strategy to keep my interest over several plays.  Your character pretty much determines your strategy, so it just comes down to getting to the good spots before anyone who is using a similar strategy (ie, using a similar character).  Or else, lag behind to clean up on what others have passed.

RATING: 9
This game is all about the theme. It captures the feeling of a sightseeing trip along the East Sea Road perfectly. The art is stylised and minimalist, and complements the relaxed mood of the game. Every aspect of the game experience supports the theme, from buying mementos and meeting travellers along the road to staying at inns and buying a meal for the night. It is a light but elegant game mechanically that just about anyone can understand.

RATING: 3
Tokiado goes down as the biggest disappointment of the Essen 2012 crop in my book.  Extremely cool graphic design, with just no game behind it.  I'm actually surprised this game was submitted to a publisher; I would not have been able to shop this design to publishers with a straight face.

Basically, in Tokaido, you advance down a track of actions, which allow you to do such exciting things as set collection, set collection, set collection, draw a card that will either give you 2 or 3 points, etc.  There's just nothing novel about anything here, and all actions are roughly equal.

Truly a case of the graphics selling a game, as this thing would have never sold with a different graphic treatment.  I'll admit the game looks absolutely gorgeous, but unfortunately, it's tough to hang on my wall.

RATING: 9

I think the execution of this game is superb in terms of creating gameplay that matches its themes ridiculously well. RATING: 4
A very good looking game of moving your pawn and getting points, nearly every turn, no matter what you do. Eat some food. Get some points. Take a bath. Get some points. Look at the scenery. Get some points. And those points are so finely balanced that in one of my recent games we had a tie, seventy to seventy, broken by tie breaker. After an entire game, with high scores, all of our well thought out decisions, led us to the exact same score.

Almost enough to give one the impression that their decisions don't matter at all.

RATING: 4
I wanted to like it. The art design is great, the kind of clean adult look that I wish were more common. And the idea behind the game is interesting, that you can either move ahead for potentially better spots, but then get fewer turns. 

In practice though, the best play is to hit as many spaces as possible, so everyone stays very bunched up - this is the 4-player game I am talking about. 

So it winds up being a game with no decisions, and little differentiation between the players. As for blocking, by the end of the game, there is so much information in player tableaus that it is too much of a chore to keep track of. Meh.

RATING: 9
An incredible game. Great for gamers of all ranges, inexperienced and hardcore players.

The game is both relaxing as well as simultaneously strategic and competitive, encouraging you to plan out the risks and rewards of playing quickly for the rewards you want, vs trying to block other players in their goal strategy. I've seen the game end before with the player who was in last result in being first due to end game rewards.

Simply put, from the games I've played the game is incredibly balanced.

RATING: 7
Rated 7 after one play.

I traded for this specifically with my lunch gaming group in mind. I really like that mechanic of "choose your spot along the road" from Egizia, Francis Drake, etc. so was curious to try it with a game that plays in an hour and is approachable.

Tokaido is an odd blend of relaxing affair but just enough shortage in resources to cause some tension. It's easy to learn, ideal for the lunch group one hour time frame... time will tell if this has enough legs to generate some requests for additional plays.

RATING: 3.5
After one play, I won't be rushing back to play this game.  It appears to have multiple paths to victory, but in reality there are only one or two things worth spending your time and game currency on.  Add to that the log-jam that players' pawns are constantly in, and there are very few actual choices to be made.  And you can see fairly far ahead what things you won't be able to do by the time your turn comes around, and why there's pretty much nothing you can do about it.  The game is nice looking (although I'd hesitate to call it "beautiful" as many do) and has nice components.  But as a game...  not for me.

RATING: 4
I became interested in Tokaido because of the prevailing mechanism: a time track where the last player on a road is the one to move. This opens up several neat dilemmas on what to do on a given turn as you not only have to consider your own gains, but those of others as well. It can very well be the case that a seemingly good move is actually very bad because it increases the point [i]delta[/i] to a greater degree. Tokaido is a family version of this principle as there is nothing more to it than that: what you can do on the road is basically score points in a variety of ways: a constant but small amount, a triangular amount (in several ways), an investment for later (in several ways too), and obtaining cash; all of which (save the income action, of course) may or may not cost you money. It's not a complex puzzle by any means, but it's not totally for simpletons either: all in all very decent family material. Between experienced gamers there is far too much emphasis on playing defensively, which means that the players never let each other stray too far ahead, at least not in the 3P-game. It is simply too costly in terms of VP deltas otherwise. I found this to be a somewhat disappointing characteristic of the game; perhaps that with more players there is more competition and thus more 'risks' taken in moving ahead of the pack more. As I said, proper characteristics of family material.

In my collection there is room for games such as this, but I'm afraid Tokaido won't enter the collection just yet. For one thing there is the alluded simplicity which detracts a little from the game itself. Second is that although some of the artwork is stunning, it's not actually very user-friendly. Meals and village-collected foods are nigh indistinguishable. The icons for scenes along the road are too small to easily indentify save by their colour, which becomes a problem in poor light and for the colour vision-impaired. The point tokens are really mind-bogglingly small, to the point where one has to wonder which moron at FunForge okay'ed that idea. Related is point three: the price of the package. I am very much inclined to pay more for the artwork in itself, but not to the point where it reaches the €40-mark. That is really too much. I'm very much inclined to wait a few years and see if this game ever goes on sale and [i]then[/i] secure a copy. For €10 to €15, it would be nice to own.

Game-technically a meager 6 in the hope that there is more conflict at higher player numbers, if not a 5; with the current price subtraction by another point.

RATING: 7

RATING: 7.9
J-B-2
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.75
This is a very light but pleasant game that combines a race and some set collection.  Players are trying to decide when it's worth moving ahead to grab a crucial bonus, versus hanging back and collecting items for a bigger payoff later.  This is all combined with a turn mechanism like Thebes, where the player furthest back on the road moves first.

Cleverly, the game has intermediate inns which force players who are out ahead to wait for others to catch up.  This keeps the number of available spaces on the road small in each section, adding some great player tension.

It's essential to mention the production values which are great - stunningly beautiful art, bright colors, and the cards and board all have clear symbols to make gameplay clear.  While this game isn't a must-buy, it will go over very well for casual gamers, family, even kids.  I'm really glad we bought this one.

RATING: 6
I think of Tokaido as a piece of art. But not much of a game. It belongs framed on the wall, not on the table.

I love Antoine Bauza, but this may be one of my least favourites of his designs. I have played this game scores of times. More than many games on my Best Games of All Time list. But I have never figured out why people think it's so good.

This game always feels like it plays itself. Every play feels basically just like every other play of it. The game is straight forward. All that changes is the tiny random changes of card draw at locations. There's never anything interesting to decide while playing.

I love games with great art. But this just feels like great art with a game pasted on.

RATING: 6
Players are travelers on a journey from Kyoto to Edo.  Simple, beautifully presented game, where players move up a linear path as far as they like, and interact with the space they end on.  There are eight different spaces, each offering victory points, with bonuses awarded at the end of the game.  The rules are easily taught, and the game play is so soothing the result barely matters.  The chaos factor in the game increases with the number of players, but I found it a bit like Ticket To Ride - the result barely matters, it's simply fun to play.  

After seven plays it moves on.  Knizia does the micro turn game so much better.  We'd much rather play Africa over this.

RATING: 8

I certainly find Tokaido to be a pleasant experience, but I'm not sure it is terribly satisfying as a game.  Perhaps it would lose its other charms if it was.

RATING: 6
The game is known for it's beauty, and it lives up to that reputation, but it comes at the expense of functionality in terms of the scale of the game.  The board is just too small, and the dots are too tiny.  The meeples are crowded and block lines of sight to the spaces.   The hobbit cards aren't a big problem, but I wish the board was about 50% bigger.    It's a simple time track game, using a "point salad" approach.   There's not a lot of incentive to spread out, as everything pretty much gets you the same amount of points.   Special characters provided some much-needed variation, but also tend to pin you into to prescribed strategies.   It's a little disappointing that the gameplay doesn't quite live up to the presentation.   Takenoko is like that, too.   However, it is a pretty approachable "casual" game and good for non-gamers.

RATING: 6
Nicely presented and themed gateway game of a pilgrimage in medieval Japan.  The art and theme come across well, though some of the iconography is confusing and the player track is very tiny and compact (in relation to the size of the board) when compared to traditional game board path layouts (i.e. H&T, Snakes&Ladders, PayDay or even Monopoly).

Tokaido is diceless, so a player can freely move his token forward up the board.  Choices and strategies (mainly around set collection and token placement) do exist, but are limited since tokens cannot share the same space (though some locations have two spaces), players' movement choices are very much dependent on the previous player's decision. Although this exists to some degree in any game, players do not have the option to pass or go back, and can in effect be blocked out - which is part of the game's strategy.  As such at higher play counts, the game may be too prior player dependent and poor play by one player will heavily influence another winning. This too could happen in most games, but I could see it particularly more of an issue in Tokaido.  At 45mins though, does it matter?  It’s not a gamer’s game and games are quick, so maybe not then.  However, the game may not have the meatiness and depth to be played more than occasionally.

I haven't tried this 2p and that may offer a somewhat tougher game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
In Tokaido, each player is a traveler crossing the "East sea road", one of the most magnificent roads of Japan. While traveling, you will meet people, taste fine meals, collect beautiful items, discover great panoramas, and visit temples and wild places but at the end of the day, when everyone has arrived at the end of the road you'll have to be the most initiated traveler – which means that you'll have to be the one who discovered the most interesting and varied things.

The potential action spaces in Tokaido are laid out on a linear track, with players advancing down this track to take actions. The player who is currently last on the track takes a turn by advancing forward on the track to their desired action and taking that action. So, players must choose whether to advance slowly in order to get more turns, or to travel more rapidly to beat other players to their desired action spaces.

The action spaces allow a variety of actions that will score in different, but roughly equal, ways. Some action spaces allow players to collect money, while others offer players a way to spend that money to acquire points. Other action spaces allow players to engage in various set collections that score points for assembling those sets. Some action spaces simply award players points for stopping on them, or give the player a randomly determined action from all of the other types.

All of the actions in Tokaido are very simple, and combined with a unique graphic design, Tokaido offers players a peaceful zen mood in its play.

RATING: 6.5
Tokaido is a fairly light set collection game with a beautiful board and anime type artwork. 

My observations are that choices during a turn seem quite limited, so most of the decision making comes from deciding how far to "jump" ahead on the track to get what you really want.  Also, the characters don't seem entirely balanced.  My other comment is that the scoring markers could have been implemented better as they are unnecessarily small and fiddly.

All in all Tokaido is an enjoyable and visually appealing game that will work if you need a light and relatively short game that scales well from 3 to 5 players.

Régóta várt kipróbálásra. A szerző és a gyors játékmenet miatt nagyon érdekelt. Hallottam már rosszat is róla, és sajnos ez a rész igazolódott be. Gyakorlatilag nincs egyáltalán döntés a játékban. Nagyon előre menni sose éri meg, így egy-két akció közül választhatsz, de hogy melyikek közül azt szinte lehetetlen megjósolni egy körrel előre, mert nagyon a többiek lépésén múlik. Mindenhol a design istenítik, de én ettől se voltam elájulva. A kártyákat szinte képtelenség megkülönböztetni egymástól, és engem a tábla se nyűgözött le. Persze üdítő a mai színkavalkádban egy ilyen szinte teljesen fehér tábla, de pont ennyi ami érdekes benne.

RATING: 6
This is a pretty good game and not too difficult to learn. It can be played 2 to 5 players. You travel the Tokaido road earning points along the way by stopping at villages to collect souvenirs, having encounters, donating to temples, earning coins, collecting panoramic photographs, and eating expensive meals at the Inns.  

After playing my first 2 player game, I realized this game is amazing for what it is. It has a lot of depth and a lot of choices to make along the way. The depth will increase in complexity with more players, because you need to carefully consider your choices based on your powers, what your opponents powers are, how much money you have, how many achievements you and your opponents have, etc. Amazing design! You have to concentrate and stay focused or you'll lose your rear.

One very nice touch I really like about this game is the fact that you collect everything along the way which is essentially your score. So if there's any disputes about who won (or because someone might of made a mistake), you can literally count up your points right off the cards you collected. The proof is in the cards.

RATING: 6
Beatiful game. Love the theme. Unfortunately the gameplay just isn't that much fun and is often frustrating. Can get quite cutthroat with the right players, which makes it more interesting if not more enjoyable. I suspect the expansions improve matters, but have yet to try them.

That said, Tokaido works quite well as a family game with younger kids. Playing with my 3 and 6 y.o. they both enjoyed playing a character with a special ability and collecting stuff as they made their journey. The last-moves-first mechanism for determining turn order teaches kids that not all games are races and discourages them from running ahead. Simple scoring allows them to start calculating the value of various actions. Playing with kids doesn't much interfere with adults playing aggressively against each other. Would definitely recommend for families!

RATING: 9
Relaxing trip from Tokio to Osaka in which you buy souvenirs, eat at inns, collect panoramas and have the occasional bath. Tokaido, an easy to learn and relatively simple game, is stress free and very well-balanced. The way players advance is original and effective: the last player always goes first. The base game is very good, but I prefer to play with Crossroads, the first expansion, which gives you some great additional choices, such as betting and collecting cherry trees. The cards are beautiful as is the white board, which instills a sense of relaxation and calmness. A well worth trip.

RATING: 6
Some games use a time track to define turn order, with the player who is further behind taking the next turn. Here, the time track is the game. You decide where to stop along the road, but most spaces you just give or sell you cards of one type or another. It basically comes down to scoring various shades of set collection, with bonus points for having the most cards or being the first to complete a set. Nothing special, but it works as light family game. The "Crossroads" expansion makes it a little better. I'm not a fan of the bland artwork, which sometimes is also confusing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
 An extremely straightforward and simple game. You start to walk in a straight line which consists of several stops until you reach an inn. Depending on the type of the location, you get some points or gain a card which has a point value. The players who reached an inn wait the rest of the players to reach the inn. At the inn all players convert some money to 6 points, if possible, and continue the journey in a similar fashion. At the 4th inn the game ends and the player with the most points win the game. 

 The game has extremely pretty visuals and can be played within half an hour. It is too simple for serious gaming and too straightforward for casual playing (compared to Ticket to Ride and even Rattus). Therefore I didn't like the gameplay experience. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
This is a classic example of a decent game with excellent artwork which elevates the experience.  At it's core, this is a zen version of the classic "move forward on a path" game (ex: life, candyland), but the gameplay choices are interesting and result in defferent point-gathering opportunities: taking photos of a panorama, collecting souvenirs, donating money to the temple, eating delicious food, etc...  

Gameplay is a bit flat, but fast and fun. The neatest mechanic is turn order, with the player furthest back in the path always going next.

RATING: 6
I liked Tokaido. It doesn't really offer much mental stimulation, but has interesting/relaxing mechanics. Basically, you get points for everything. Primary player interaction is blocking and a little bit of racing toward a few point goals. My main concern is that although I actually like the game's play style, there also isn't much variation in the overall experience from game to game.

In the end, I believe Tokaido is worth playing once or twice, but it certainly isn't worth owning. There just isn't much game actually in the box and not all that much of anything else with major staying power either.

RATING: 7
(06/22/2013) Comment based on one play.

Very light, yet very elegant and pleasant game to play with non-gamer friends and family. The movement is reminiscent of Glen More, where the last player on the track moves first. Move too far ahead to get the space/action you want, and you sit and wait as the others catch up. Move too slowly, and you may be giving up valuable spaces just ahead of you to the other players. There is some blocking in terms of spaces and cards drawn during meals, but other than that, the theme and the game play fit well together--take a leisurely stroll along the Japanese countryside and collect varied, but wonderful memories.

The artwork is very nice and well done. The unfolding panoramic views, as the players visit scenic vistas is a nice game mechanic that contributes strongly to the theme. Rules are pretty straightforward. A pleasant little game, that seems to have come in bit oversized box. Initial rating: "7" with room to move up a point.

RATING: 8.5
Where do I start? This game is simply great, I love the thematic and the turn order is easy, as in the real world you are waiting for the slowest player hahaha. The game may be look a bit confusing at the beginning but it is simple. As many games, you must score the most points to win and every stop on the game helps you a bit in scoring.

The down on the game that I found is when you play it with 5 players. The thing is that sometimes you may never stop on a certain type of stop because other players may block you, even the 2 players stops may get full. So, it may be a bit frustrating.

Anyway, I like the game, it is good and I recommend it, just I don't play it as often as I may like because when we gather to play we are 6 players.

----------------------

¿Por donde comienzo? El juego simplemente es excelente, me fascina la temática y el orden de los turnos es sencillo, así como en la vida real, estás esperando al jugador más lento jajaja. El juego puede parecer un poco confuso al inicio pero realmente es sencillo. Como en muchos juegos, el objetivo para ganar es obtener la mayor cantidad de puntos y cada parada te ayuda proporcionándote puntos.

Lo que no me gusta del juego ocurre al jugar con 5 jugadores. El problema es que a veces no es posible que te detengas en un determinado tipo de parada ya que los otros jugadores las ocupen, incluso las que tienen capacidad para dos jugadores. Lo cual puede ser molesto para el jugador al que le suceda.

El juego me gusta, es bueno y lo recomiendo, solamente que no lo puedo jugar porque nos reunimos 6 personas y ya no es posible jugarlo.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
First, this game is gorgeous. Second, we have (too) many non-gamer friends and relatives, so Tokaido comes out often as a gateway game.

There's strategy -- deciding how to maximize your total movements -- and tactics -- deciding how to make up for ruined plans.

Yes, this game can be played as a simple hop-and-collect journey, but we find it becomes a cutthroat experience where you hate-block your opponents at every move, or try and quickly gather up a certain resource -- card, item, visit, etc. -- before your opponents.

I don't always wish to play Tokaido, but I don't mind it when a new guest prompts it to hit our table.

-----

Note: I only started tracking in January of 2017, so please ignore the play count.

RATING: 7
Light set collection game with players' movement along a track ordered by a variable last-player-on-the track-goes-next turn order mechanism.

One 4-player play only so far, which everyone enjoyed. You're competing for resources and blocking rather than clobbering each other, and while on some turns your move is pretty obvious, the canny player will recognise that reading what the others are likely to do and planning a few turns ahead can pay dividends.

Very attraactive aesthetics suit the mood of the game very well, and while it may not be the main attraction on a games night it does make an enjoyable change from confrontational wargames and dry, efficiency engine Euros.

RATING: 7
TL;DR 7/10 because the random mechanics make it nigh impossible to strategize for at least 2/5 of the players every game no matter what they do. The bonuses are not worth the effort made focusing on doing ONE THING to complete the tasks that reward them, when you could just try to do a general mix of things and screw other people over more by blocking their moves. You'll likely be blocked out of doing that one thing quite often anyways depending on what you go for, so strategy and getting bonuses both are quite broken and should've been made better in beta testing...

OTHERWISE: it's a very neat game!  We end up being QUITE silly while narrating our games in my apartment and we do enjoy the game overall. It is quite fun even when you're not doing so well, but I would like some sort of shift in rules (maybe not randomly choosing a traveler would help heaps?) to make it more fair, as losing 3 games in a row really sucks (it feels like the economy or the card game asshole, where if you get stuck in the bottom 2 early on, you're stuck there the whole game).


----------------------------------------------
1) some travelers powers are constantly screwed over by the obligatory, complete and utterly random way the travelers are chosen and what your friends will choose to do each game, on top of the randomly chosen starting order (there's no rule on this, we draw meeples from a dice bag as house-rule) and how everyone but the free temple donations traveler tries to skip temples and screw other people over. Also the person who gets that traveler can pretty much not give a crap what they do as they're likely going to get enough points from the temple and doing whatever is available for who cares amount of points to easily out-earn everyone (after seeing two different roommates play this character in different ways).
If there was more balance to the abilities/bonuses and the ability to actually benefit from them (most times you're just plain screwed and no amount of strategy will save you) I would enjoy this game more. It's too damn unfair/broken because of the luck of the draw. It feels like the card game asshole, and whoever gets the first few "good spots" is like the king and queen and will likely stay on top (or at least top 3) all the game.

2) With the randomly chosen travelers, you're very very likely to constantly get screwed over by other people landing on the spaces that your travelers benefits from, depending completely on the order in which you began. I wish I could suggest starting first, last or somewhere in the middle, but this is honestly a crap shoot and even when you try to make a plan, you're pretty locked into your position in the game by the time you hit the first inn/hotel. There is no way to strategize outside of "what can I do next turn based on what everyone else MIGHT MAYBE POSSIBLY do". you can really only predict that people will probably paint and take the banks immediately, and anyone not painting is going to be a hot springs whore.

3) Getting a hero based on "meeting a friend" seems to suck the most as most people, when not painting (and NOBODY shops [much] in my experience after three 5-person games) will automatically choose hot springs and meet a friend (instant points in most cases and not having to hit up a temple). Travelers that get to buy things at the shop (villages?) for cheap also seem to get screwed because unless you have a ton of cash from banks, you can't afford food AND shopping (and food is more worthwhile overall for the price per points [1 coin for 6 points? yes plz!]).

4) Buying things in the shops seems utterly useless unless you have tons of cash and also are lucky enough to get every bank space and meet the "noble" friend once or twice, on top of having one of the people who has a nice buying ability (get second cheaper item free if you can afford both when buying 2). After playing this character, watching a roommate play it, and seeing a third roommate play another shops-based traveler, my opinion is they're USE. LESS.

5) This utter imbalance in the game is possibly why it has only 7.1 on the site. It has great reviews and I do like the game, my roommates also like it even when they don't do as well. I'm sure we will get better with time (we've had it for about 4 days and have played 2 times so far) but as geek veterans to board and role games, it was a bit disappointing that the inventors didn't beta this a bit more and try to balance it so that no matter what you chose, you at least stand a chance by strategizing to fuck over other people by taking what THEY need. At this point, even trying to do that wont work for every traveler because then you're probably not doing anything actually useful to help yourself stack points/get bonuses.

6) BONUSES? Yeah... right! The bonuses for doing the most of something? 3 freakin points! That's it?!! You should get at LEAST 6-10 for being the person to have the most friends or most expensive meals. GOD DAMN NOODLES FOR 1 COIN GIVES YOU MORE POINTS FOR THE EFFORT THAN PAINTING. And from my experience, nobody can finish 2 different paintings first (for the 6 points that would be worth) anyways, unless your friends are seriously uninterested in it and are buying items and food and hot springs/friends-ing it up (but in my experience everyone paints at least a bit to avoid spaces they dont want like the temples or to try and "count cards" to get to a space they need).

7) The shopping and food buying can really put you out if you dont start with 6-9 coins and hit up at least 2 banks. The bonuses from doing it compared to the amount you waste letting other people finish their objectives instead of screwing them over to get ahead just to get those measly 3 stupid points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I was interested, in a detached intellectual way, about how simply moving to one of the next available spots on a path constitute a meaningful game decision. The game is easy to explain. Players are on a big path. The player in back moves to one of the available spaces in front of them. Spaces involve collecting cards of various types in order to get points and/or build sets of cards in order to... get points! There are essentially two things to think about as you play - do you skip spaces to ensure you get what you need most or to block people from getting what they need AND do you have enough money to purchase the upcoming cards that you need. There are only a couple of ways to collect money and it is pretty important to have some throughout the game as a number of cards or actions have a cost to perform. Players take on personas that give a unique special ability which creates some differentiation.

The game feels pretty unique, and I give it credit for that, but game play is one-dimensional and not terribly fun. It's not that I dislike it, but I'd rather play most any of my other family games.

RATING: 4
Almost no game here. It looks like a Guardian info-graphic, but that is sadly the best thing about the game.

The movement / turn order has no tension due to obtaining points for everything.

luck will decide which end of the 10% variance in score you come out on - will you draw a useful villager? Will you get a 2 point or 3 point hot springs card? Will you draw items that make a set when you visit the shop?

This game is flaccid and my friends who played rated at 7 initially, but it has dropped a rank after every subsequent play.

The most fun to be had was doing ironic drum-rolls followed by cheers/jeers when a player was drawing a hot spring...

Has its place in my collection - there's no other game that gives me the same feeling for now.

RATING: 7
After getting my first Antoine Bauza game for my birthday in 2016, in 2017 I got another one, namely Tokaido. My brother got it at an inflated price, since it is unfortunately not available in germany. I wanted this as another "gateway game" for people who usually don't play too much. I think this might work better at an introductory level than Takenoko, but still depending on whom I play with, this still turns out to be a little harder to grasp for some people than I would have thought. Overall it left a good impression though and the first two plays were mostly fun experiences.

RATING: N/A
In Tokaido, each player is a traveler crossing the "East sea road", one of the most magnificent roads of Japan. While traveling, you will meet people, taste fine meals, collect beautiful items, discover great panoramas, and visit temples and wild places but at the end of the day, when everyone has arrived at the end of the road you'll have to be the most initiated traveler – which means that you'll have to be the one who discovered the most interesting and varied things.

RATING: 4
As a board game for kids, maybe it could be fun, but I am still not really sure. This game simply lack of adventure.

The movement mechanic is interesting but that's it. In the end, all the actions you do will give you victory points in one way or the other so that at the end, every body has in average the same amount of points.

There are a few events that are OK, but collecting pieces of land scape, I just don't understand it. Not only it's not fun but it makes no sense at all.

I know it's not a kill monster game, but give to my character some interesting adventures to do. For me to plan getting protection to pass certain hasard ahead. 

So yes, the idea to travel and the movement mechanic is very interesting and new. But what you do is simply boring.

Another important thing: THE BOX IS HUGE. Seroiusly, I don't think the game components takes more than 20% of the box's space. I would be curious to see if somebody could measure that up. It might not be the best wasted space box game, but it's surely in the top 10.

RATING: 7
A relaxed set collection game, Tokaido is a good starting game that introduces interesting game systems without being overwhelming. Featuring gorgeous art that enlivens its medieval japanese setting, Tokaido seeks to capture the feeling of being on vacation and it does so surprisingly well, accenting the game with failed itineraries, memorable unplanned encounters, good food, and a lot of troubles with parking. Plays up to 5 players.

Game by Antoine Bauza, designer of 7 Wonders, Ghost Stories etc.  Quite a light game about travelling across Japan along the Tokaido road from Kyoto to Edo. There's some set collection and jostling for limited positions along the road to pick up points, collect souvenirs and meals at inns. Not a particularly deep game, but fun - and the artwork is superb.

RATING: 7.5
Not a bad game at all.  Pretty light.  Beautiful, colorful board and components.  I really like the idea of deciding whether to jump forward to the ideal spot to secure it, or get more things along the way that offer fewer points.  That specific element reminds me of the placement of boats in Egizia.  But yeah, not bad.  Fun, light, beautiful.  One note.  If you breathe in too heavy you might accidentally swallow one of the point counters.

RATING: 7.8
Essen 2012 with dedication from Naïade

Played it with my parents: first thing my parents said: what a beautiful game! And this is the first game my mother immediately said she really likes playing the game.

It's a very accessible game, very easy to understand. It's beautiful to see and the idea of 'zen' is worked out very well. At some time I think the novelty will wear off: I might not want to play it too often. There is a bit of tactical thinking (blocking spaces) and strategic thinking (money management) and getting the most out of your character, but that's it. It's mostly a game to, just, ...do.

Edit: my wife (again ;)) also says that the game is very beautiful and that she isn't yet done playing... Also a gaming friend commented that this is a beautiful game and it is really nice to play it. Not to win, but just to play: he said it has simple rules, simple decisions, so... very Zen!

Edit 2: my wife requests this game a lot (more than 7 Wonders) and she wins a lot. That's good! My opinion about the game is starting to change: with some characters you will win, with others you won't... I haven't played enough to make this a hard statement, but the hardness of the statement is growing. Winning is of course not the purpose of this game and there is never a feeling that you are the loser, even if you lost. Playing this game after a hectic game is very satisfying: it really calms you down.

Edit 3: my gamer friend even requests it (I didn't bring it to our movie/game/pinball night) and wonders why there isn't much written about this game in the gameing media. He still requests the game regularly because of the beautiful art of the game alone...

Edit 4: the 'new' wore off. The game gets played only now and then if we want something relaxing on the table. Otherwise the game doesn't really provide a challenge (it gets better with the expansion, but not by much). These are my wife's words.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Absolutely love this game. It should really get a lot more love than it gets. For a zen-like, relaxing experience, the base game is perfect (the art contributes to that for sure). 

For a more involved, strategic game, add in the Crossroads expansion. This "10" rating is contingent on using the expansion. Without it, the game is still probably a "9". It is wonderful.

RATING: 8
Everyone I've played this with so far thinks this game is weird. It kinda is. The rules are extremely simplistic. The board is, in essence, a straight line. The goal is to have the most pleasant journey. Make some good friends, see some good sites, eat some good food.

And yet it *can* be a bit mean. People blocking your spots. How dare you paint that picture! *I* was going to paint that picture! Oh my gosh! Do you really need another friend! I need a friend... look at me, I'm friendless!

It's that combination of meanness and serenity that makes it an interesting game. 

Good art, interesting theme, perfect playing time, and simple rules that provide just enough meat to keep you interested throughout the game. 

Played: 12 or so times with many more online plays.

RATING: 6.5
On design this is an absolute 10.

With 2 players, there is definitly something there. The dummy-player that you get to control adds a lot of this kind of screwage.

With 4 players, I do agree it needs something else, as the game plays itself to a good degree.


Either way, it's a very light gateway game. But since it plays quickly, lightly, and looks pretty, it's definitly something to break out at the start of an evening.

RATING: 7
As a traveller in Imperial Japan, you go to different locations to build up your collection of cards. The trick with this game is that the board is one straight line to finsih points, and you can never go backwards.  So you have to trade off leaving things behind to get what you want.

It's a set collection game of panoramas, people, meals and so forth. It's a fun game, and a bit hard to analzye. I've only played 3 player, but I think it may play better with 4 or 5 players, as some areas allow 2 travellers. It's a neat mechanic, and some of the choices are really tough.

RATING: 7
After playing this game many times I still enjoy it every time.  There is technically a winner, but each player travels their own path (although what that path can entail is influenced by your co-travelers), having their own experience.  A beautiful, meandering game that leaves one feeling refreshed and happy if you play with the intention of experiencing the journey and even periodically allow for a co-traveller to gain something they were hoping for, or perhaps incredibly frustrated and angry if you play to get the most stuff (it is after all a set collection game). 

Perhaps a bit of a metaphor for human life?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Molto di questo gioco lo fa la grafica molto accattivante, snella ed elegante. Il gioco invece risulta molto semplice, ai limiti del ripetitivo, e le dinamiche strategiche non sono complesse. L'autore di Hanabi, 7 Wonders e Tokenoko presenta questo gioco adatto ai babbani che però si lascia giocare volentieri tanto da farmelo considerare un piccolo gioiellino. I punti di forza sono la mancanza di tempi morti, la durata giusta (contenuta), la tematica della passeggiata ben rappresentata e lo spirito meditativo orientale. A livello strategico il gioco è poco profondo anche se non manca una sufficiente interazione. Eccelso lavoro di marketing che offre un gioco con poco materiale, in alcuni casi nemmeno funzionale (i segna punti microscopici) in una scatola sproporzionata ad un prezzo abbastanza alto. I disegni e l'elganza però rendono questo gioco invitante come molto simpatico è lo sviluppo delle carte paesaggio. La versione base è un gioco rilassante adatto probabilmente ai meno competitivi. La mia valutazione sul gioco cresce se gli si affianca anche l'espansione Crossroad che a mio avviso lo completa e lo rende più fruibile anche da giocatori più assidui senza sconvolgerlo ma regalanso un po' più di interattività rendendo ogni scelta meno ovvia e banale.

RATING: 6
[b]Tokaido[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]Dropped my rating because after reflecting on my one play I realized that as awesome as the artwork is and zen the play experience can be there just isn’t enough going on to entice me to play it again. The decisions seem too straightforward which, in my opinion, would make repeated plays somewhat boring. Not a game I’d refuse to play again, but also not one I’m seeking out. [/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I really had a great time playing this game, it was completely laid back and chill. We played with the Crossroads expansion and to be honest I think I would enjoy just playing the base game. I wouldn't go into a game of Tokaido looking for depth of strategy, in my opinion it is an experience game. You truly feel like you are walking along this path and experiencing the journey. 

When you are looking for a game to play after a particularly stressful day and you need to relax take Tokaido off the shelf, sit back and enjoy a sublime experience.[/i]

RATING: 6
"Gorgeous production, and I love the time track mechanism. But it's quite cutthroat with 2-3p. Ignore those who say there are no decisions."

What I like:
- I love the elegant simplicity (of the game and the art)
- The "time track" mechanism is one of my favorites
- The game only lasts about a dozen turns (45-60 minutes)
- It rewards specializing, but makes that hard

What I don't like:
- Some icons can be hard to understand
- Some majority scoring, which isn't my favorite
- Official 2p rules allow for "mean" play, so I'll use a random variant
- It's a bit of a "point salad" game
- The insert looks good, but doesn't quite work as designed
- I wish the box were a standard size

It seems that the serene atmosphere aligns well with the gameplay. Each turn, you basically have to decide whether to take the next available spot, or to jump ahead just a bit to get something you really want.

2p is quite cutthroat, and 3p is somewhat cutthroat. It's probably most open with 4, and still fairly open with 5.

I think the BGG-voted 1.7 weight is reasonable.

Tokaido is a casually-paced game about sight-seeing in old world Japan.  Players leisurely make their way down the game's path, stopping often to visit the various types of sites.  However, you can't go backwards, you can't go where someone else is, and you're constrained by money, which adds a little bit of urgency to make sure you can visit the sites that are important to your strategy.  On the whole, though, this is a very relaxing game that's not too deep, but has enough strategy to satisfy.

RATING: N/A
A competitive point-scoring game with a clever lead-and-follow system.  You and your opponents are travelers in ancient Japan on the famous Tokaido, a scenic route across the country.  Your goal is to sample the cuisine, see the sights, buy some souvenirs, and make your trip the most memorable.  An ingenious rules mechanic allows you to move as far as you wish on any turn - with the caveat that the next turn is ALWAYS taken by the hindmost player.  Be as greedy or as cautious as you want, it balances out very neatly.

RATING: 5
A beautiful but unsatisfying game. The art is very nice, but the gameplay is not terribly interesting. There are some hard decisions to make about whether to make sure you get a spot you really want or take an earlier spot and hope no one else takes the one you want before you get to it. This encourages the kind of "stop and calculate all the possibilities" way of playing that I really don't enjoy, and it slows down what really should be a fast game. The scoring is also very fiddly and calculation-heavy for a game with fairly light decisions. It's trying to be a beautiful, laid-back experience but the scoring breaks that. However, even if it had succeeded in being a laid-back game, that just isn't what I'm looking for in a game.

RATING: N/A
31.0 x 31.0 x 7.5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
What a gem. This is a work of art. The game is very calm, beautiful and mellow. The theme it's integrated superbly, you can even learn few facts about Japan from the manual.

Rules are relatively simple. You're just a traveler on your journey, and your goal isn't to be there first, but to amass good amount of great experiences and items - be it panorama views, encounters with locals, souvenirs from a village, or a bath in a hot spring. All these things grants you points during the game and you have resources (money) that are needed to control some of them. The game itself is just going forward, and the only mechanic here is to block others and realize your plan. You also have to adjust during the game if someone blocks your next desired visit destination. You'll learn something about Japan on the way, too - the names, meals, items - it stays with you.

I was quite surprised after the first play. I expected a child's play, judging from photos and manual, but it turned out to be a very nice, peaceful and aesthetically pleasing experience. This is not a game to play over and over, but once in a while, I suppose it's great. You play it not to win, but just for the experience. If counting points during the game is too troublesome and causes some analysis paralysis, then don't do that. All the things generating points stay with you, so you can as well count points by the end of the game, turning it into a possible surprise and anticipation.

It costs a little bit too much.

And last but not least, I absolutely love the minimalistic design of the box.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I just don't think the gameplay or the art design/theme is as strong as people make them out to be.
Gameplay: a) While the simplistic gameplay is accessible to new gamers, the point valuation of each move isn't very understandable until after they've played multiple times. I guess as long as they don't care about losing, it could be considered a gateway game. 
b) The actions and character abilities are supposed to be roughly equal from each other, but I don't agree. Getting a weak character to start with puts you immediately behind another player, easily 5 points-worth by the end of the game.
c) The random element of the gift shop pisses me off. Spending 5 precious actions during the game, thereby seeing 15 cards, and not even being able to make one set (of 4) is a colossal game design mistake for a strategic game. It's very similar to Thebes' mistake, where it doesn't matter how many resources you strategically invest, you can easily come up empty.

Art design/theme: While the minimalist pastel color/art scheme invokes ancient Japan, there's something missing. It's just so flat and card-based. If they could have incorporated some figures or other 3-d elements it would improve greatly.
While the gameplay does suggest a trip, wandering from location to location based on what's crowded or not, I just felt I was optimizing points. Especially the art locations, it felt solely a point/set-collection exercise, not a vacation/trip/journey.

Tactical take-aways:
Don't fight other players for locations if you can help it. Like so many other games, if you are competing directly with someone else, you will be expending too many resources for those points.
Try to focus on the locations that aren't being fought over.
I believe the monkey pools are a more reliable source of points. Typically 2.5 points per visit, plus the achievement for 3 more.
The art locations are 2 points per visit, 2.5 points per, and 3 points per and require 3, 4, and 5 visits each respectively.

Oh, and it simply doesn't make sense to score as you go in this game - just carefully add the points from the cars at the end of the game.

RATING: 5
I want to love this game. I really do. The artwork is fantastic, the laidback and "experience beauty" theme is so so clear.

But it is boring. So so boring. And for that matter it seems that the rules about initial coins are never enough to garnish points past the second village. It seems on a "to win" point of view, the best way to win is to just visit every damn scenery you can as the most points given is through such.

A very slow, but beautiful game though - and to be fair, the objective is to enjoy every artwork and a relaxing time to not worry about the points. It does that well, but it is very little "game" to Tokaido. It is like "The Game of Life" but easier, and less interesting, with better art.

RATING: 8

I think the main reason it is not as well recognized as it should is a common missconception about how light this game is. And for light gamers who don't like to plan ahead not only for yourself but also for your enemies this game seem very luck-driven. In my opinion they just missed target of their otherwise great and beautiful game.

RATING: 7.5
It's odd, but this is the kind of game where you're actually a bit surprised to get exactly what you're expecting. The whole thing really is just a relaxing walk to Edo with your friends.
There's a small amount of strategy to the game, and yes, there will be a "winner", but Tokaido is almost more like background "music" to a social event. It will probably be over before you know it.
Overall: It's really more of an experience than a game. It feels weird storing Tokaido on my game shelf because I never want it judged as such. Very neat.

RATING: 7
I have only played this once, so maybe I'll change my mind with subsequent plays.  My initial impressions are below.

Pros :
Very nice artwork

The board is very "clean" and looks inviting.

The theme is very original.

Cons :
Despite looking great, the cards seem made of lower quality materials than I would expect.  I was worried the cards would crease if I tried to riffle shuffle them.

I felt the game kind of played itself.  I won the game, but I can't explain how I did.  There is some strategy in blocking an opponent, but then you often cause yourself not to capitalize on your own advantages.  Typically I just tried to take what was given and move along the board as slowly as possible to get the most turns.

RATING: 3.5
I felt too limited in what I was able to do... Constantly.  The theme is great. The art is fantastic. 

You end up being locked out of so many places that it's pretty obvious what your best option is each turn.

You can end up having little to no currency for the majority of the game because other people will inevitably take those locations before you can get there.

I won't ever play this game again.

No, there are not a lot of critical choices. However, even with those strikes against it its exactly what it sets out to be – an enjoyable shared journey. We’ve decided that Tokaido is a vacation simulator. There are a ton of things you want to do on your vacation but you can’t possibly do all of them so the way to have the best vacation ever is to focus on just the right activities.

RATING: 5

-big board

2-5 Player:

RATING: 3
Everyone already knows this is a beautiful game with an original and lovely theme, etc. What matters is the game play, which has been - fairly - compared to Candyland. On your turn you go to the next available space that makes any sense for you (i.e. if you have money you go to the next space, otherwise you go to the next space you can afford), draw a card and do what it says. The only clear decision is whether to gamble spending money now for a few points against the possibility that you won't have enough for a few more points next round. That's it.

Defenders of this game say that the other decision - whether to skip forward more a few spaces to get something special (in exchange for giving your opponent extra turns) - is where the heart of the game lies, but from what I have seen I call bull. In the comparable game - Glen More - that decision makes sense, because you are getting something very highly valued in exchange for the turns you give up; sometimes 18 or more points, and each additional turn you gave up is also -3 points for the person who takes it. In this game, the MOST you will get by jumping forward a few spaces is 3 extra points, and EVERY turn nets you 2-5 points, so it is NEVER worthwhile to lose turns.

I would be happy to be proven otherwise, but until then this remains a thing of beauty that will I hope to never play again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Played once with 3P. Tokaido is an oppressively pleasant game to play. It revolves around a [thing=66362][/thing]-esque turn order track, which is great, except that every single location gives you VP of some sort. (Well, the Farm is an exception since it gives you coins. But coins are eventually converted to VP anyway.) Consequently, there is no incentive to jump too far ahead on the track to scoop up points, because opponents who lag behind can pick up just as much VP. 

To be fair, there are opportunities for interaction under the veneer of niceness. It is worthwhile getting into the Inn early if your opponents lack coins, or taking up a coveted slot to deny an opponent a point or two. The trouble is, most casual Euros can accomplish the same thing and still offer a deeper experience. Tokaido has a niche as a family-friendly game, but there's not much it can ultimately offer beyond its amazing aesthetics (not counting that glitch of a score track) and gentle aura.

RATING: 10
Appearance: Infinity/10
Mechanics: 10/10
Clarity: 9.5/10
Pieces: moderate

Get this game. Period. 
It became my personal favorite the moment I opened it. This hidden gem is all about the journey. The winner is the player that has had the most fulfilling experience traveling between two historical cities in old Japan. Hot springs, souvenir shops, and other wonderful experiences are waiting to be had. I would describe this as a mellow mood game, but if players decide to sabotage (most commonly by occupying spaces other players get perks for) it can escalate to Mario Kart levels of competition. 

I cannot stress enough how beautiful the art is. The characters are so memorable and distinct, the meals at the inns look delicious, and the panoramas are gorgeous. The game board looks deceptively bare compared to most games but the wide expanses really instill a sense that you're traveling a great distance. 

I have absolutely nothing bad to say about this game, except maybe if you somehow lost the rulebook you might not be able to identify the effects of certain cards. None of them have text written on them, but rather pictures of what they do and those can be hard to decipher sometimes.

Overall this is a must have, go get one, like right now.

RATING: 6
1 play, all newbies

Yeah, it's kinda pretty, but it's not at all what I was expecting based on all the hype! I thought I'd see something more simple, more like Japanese line drawings or something. It has a nice board, but I'm not nearly as enthusiastic about it as others seem to be. The icons are also kind of on the teeny weenie side!

Game play, too, is just okay. It didn't seem to any of us that there were a lot of choices, really: mostly, you chose based on turn order, what your special power was, and what few spaces were available before the next inn. Clearly more choices may appear with repeated play as we might learn more nuances in the game, but I doubt any of us are excited to play this one again. Too light for its own good! Maybe it would be better with fewer players, and with a different audience.

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
This is a set-collection game with a Japanese theme.

The board shows a road with several types of stops (some with multiple places, for use with larger player counts). Players start the game with a unique character, which will generally give them some kind of bonus associated with one of these location types. Players' meeples all start on one end, and will be moving one way down the path. The player furthest back always moves next, to a free spot. Each spot can only accommodate one token, so people will be skipping different spots. The inns, though, are required stops -- people will pause there until everyone else has caught up, and then begin forward progress again.

Each of the stops has an action (donate cash to the temple) or a different deck (most) associated with it. The decks have different types of cards: 
- collect parts of 1 of 3 types of panoramas, gradually (hopefully) finishing it; earn VP with each new card. There are bonus VP awarded to the first player to complete each panorama.
- simply earn some VP right away
- buy souvenirs, for different costs and worth different VP
- etc.

At the inn, players may buy food cards (must sample different type at every inn); the earlier players get more choices (draw n+1 cards; earlier to arrive choose first).

Once everyone reaches the final inn, there are end-game bonuses for having the most of each of the different card types, and for rank of contributions to the temple. Most VP wins!

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a pretty accessible game about players going on a tourist vacation through Japan.  I have taken one trip to Japan myself and loved it there, the culture, the food, the atmosphere, it was all very unique and enjoyable.  This game does a pretty decent job of mimicking that and making a game out of it.

The gameplay itself is very simple.  Each turn players move as many spaces as they like and choose any available space and take the action for that space.  It can be many different things, most of which provide points, such as buying souvenirs, visiting a temple, or painting a picture.  Gameplay continues like this until all players have reached the end of the map and then tally scores.

The simple gameplay itself is what soured me a bit on an otherwise very pretty and interestingly themed game.  There is no benefit to finishing first.  Getting to each inn first gives you more options of which food to buy and you can't buy the same food more than once but it seemed like there was enough of a variety that this wasn't that big of a deal.  There was a little bit of pushing and shoving with taking spaces other players might want right before their turn but for the most part you want to move as short a distance as possible each turn to get the most stuff making the decisions not very deep or interesting.

I've played similar "move as far as you like" games such as Francis Drake but there is always an added benefit to skipping spaces, such as in Francis Drake where the first person to most spots gets greater benefits (or sometimes even the only benefit) out of that space and finishing the entire action track first gives you better turn position in the second phase.  Here the actions are always the same no matter when you get to them and the only benefit of getting places first in a greater selection from an already pretty large pool.

The game does look very nice.  The artwork is stylish and pretty and does evoke a Japanese aesthetic.  It is probably a very good game for very casual/simple gamers but not something I'm over the moon about.

RATING: 3.5
That's 3.5 points for the artwork - the game itself is awful.

I blind bought it for the great graphics but all that did was teach me to not blind buy ever again.

Despite what one might think it is not a good family game. It's too simple even for that (my mother wondered why I bought such a dull game which tells me a lot about Tokaido...). In my experience it's also significantly worse with 5 players than with 3. With 3 players, there are at least some decisions to be made, with 5 - nope, nothing. The board actually has more options in the 5-player variant but obviously that doesn't help at all - it just makes the game take longer...

I also have some complaints about the components: the pieces used to keep track of the points are ridiculously small. If one of those things falls off the table you'll never find it again.
The scoring track has the point values ordered in a completely unintuitive zig-zag way. Why would you do that!?
The box inlay is odd - the box is huge but the inlay has very small pockets, too small for all the cards for instance. Why!?

My impression is that Funforge wanted to turn the awesome artwork into a game asap and then rushed it. This game - both rules and components wise - was not sufficiently tested.

RATING: 8
Tokaido is a lovely game. It is simple, light, and excellent for gamers who aren't interested in long, fiddly affairs.

Tokaido is a gorgeous piece of visual art, too, and there's a surprising bit of depth to the play. It is a game of competative math set in a fluid, carefree package that just whizzes by in a flash. This is a game that I could see being easily overplayed as the depth of play isn't matched with a depth and variety of experience. But it's so short and delightful, I'd expect to play it twice everytime it hits the table. :)

Unique, well thought-out, incredible aesthetic design, exceptional merging of form and function in the components ... everything about it is top class.

Note: I hate some minor component issues with the coins. They didn't punch out very nicely as the cardboard was too pliable and not cut-through enough. Two of them ended up ruined. The other components all faired well though and I don't anticipate running out of coins in the bank so I'm not going to bother replacing the broken ones. Also the insert is a little befuddling but I've gotten used to that by now and am instead surprised by an insert that both fits everything well and makes organizational sense.

RATING: 5.3
Disappointingly dull, with the decisions you make having very little outcome on the final result; unless you do something stupid like jumping ahead 5 spaces from everyone else. In which case you'll guarantee that you'll lose.

Each space is worth, broadly speaking, the same value, so the key to winning is basically to take lots of micro steps and move forward as few spaces as possible and hope the hot springs or the people you meet on the way don't screw your chances.

It's hard to hate because it's just so inoffensive and it *is* very pretty (if a little infographic-y), but I saw nothing of interest.

RATING: 7
A fun game, I could see this really working well for families.  Basically you are exploring Tokaido to collect things and have a better traveling experience than the other players.  When I last played this the min-maxer in the group absolutely dominated the score.  I am a bit fearful this game is easy to have snowball but other than that it has great art, theme, and fun.

Update: I am decreasing this game score a bit.  If you play with someone who maximizes the move mechanic and other player's are not competitive the player maximizing moves is going to win this game hands down.  It is unfortunate such a specific strategy can win so frequently, it seems like the move mechanic in the game is effectively broken.  The game is at best a 7 for me now, I think with the right crowds this can still be enjoyable but with serious board gamers you might find this frustrating.

RATING: 6
Exceptionally pleasant game. I'd even describe it as charming. It's light, but it isn't trying to be anything but. Still, the ability to block other players adds a touch of welcome cut-throat, and you do need to be smart about optimizing your actions. For a meatier game with a similar action selection mechanic, try Egizia or Glen More. Yet if you prefer a game that goes well with families or socialization, stick with Tokaido.

RATING: 8.1
I've heard it referred to as a 'Zen' game, and that it is. There are points everywhere, and therefore scoring isn't a tricky feat. Money however, is a bit tighter, and there is enough difficulty in trying to work out how far down the track you should go to either block opponents from getting good actions (such as money, a favourite trick of mine particularly in the two player variant) or whether a later action is more valuable to you than smaller ones along the way. And there is always the risk you might get blocked, too. 

Sure it's a borrowed mechanic, and I have a suspicion I would like Egizia more, but this is a nice rendition of the system also.

RATING: 8
2-5 players.

Set collection game that almost plays as a worker placement game.
You are a traveler walking from Kyoto to Tokyo and want to have the best journey out of everyone. Player the furthest from the end moves, but each space can be blocked so  players may find their selves passing stops along the track trying to reach their own goals, but moving too far forward gives everyone else more options or even turns.

Pretty chill but features some nice decision making.

Also it looks freaking so sweet. That art style man.
